Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ UTM zone ♣ 14-Q ♥

♠ Peak Date ♣ ♥

Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 500 BCE-300 BCE ♥ The start of this period is marked by the founding of Monte Albán at the centre of the three valleys.

Start: 500 BCE

"Monte Alban, founded circa 500 BC at the nexus of the valley's three branches, was one of highland Mesoamerica’s earliest cities, and it remained the most populous and architecturally monumental settlement in the Southern Highlands for more than a millennium (Blanton, 1978)[1]

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ ♥ probably unknown. The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[2]

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ ♥ probably unknown. It has been suggested that Monte Alban became the new centre of a confederation of people from different settlements of the valley, but the exact relationship between Monte Alban and the remaining settlements of the valley is not known.[3]

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ MxRosar ♥ The largest centre before Monte Alban was San Jose Mogote, and it has been hypothesised that much of the population of Monte Alban came from San Jose Mogote and its surrounding settlements in the Etla subvalley. [4]
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ population migration ♥ People migrated to occupy the new settlement, Monte Alban, during this period, although material culture (including ceramic and architectural styles) suggest cultural continuity from the previous period.[5][6]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ MxAlb1L ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ ♥
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ ♥

♠ Capital ♣ Monte Albán ♥ Monte Albán was the largest settlement in the Valley of Oaxaca at this time, and was founded at the beginning of this period with an estimated population of 5,000 people.[7] This settlement is taken to be the centre of the Zapotec polity, with populations originating from other settlements in the valley.

♠ Language ♣ Zapotec ♥ “…the iconography and hieroglyphic writing of Monte Albán I suggest that we are dealing with people who spoke an early version of Zapotec and practiced an early form of Zapotec religion.”[8]
♠ Linguistic Family ♣ Otomanguean ♥ Both the Zapotecan (Zapotec and Chatino) and Mixtecan (Mixtec, Cuicatec and Trique) languages belong to the Otomanguean language family, which also includes: Otopamean, Chinantecan Popolocan, Huave, Amuzgo and Manguean language groups.[9]
♠ Language Genus ♣ ♥
♠ Religious Tradition ♣ ♥
♠ Religion Family ♣ ♥
♠ Religion Sect ♣ ♥


Map[edit]

General Description[edit]

The Monte Albán Early I phase runs from 500 to 300 BCE. The settlement at Monte Albán was founded at the beginning of this period and located in what was previously a 'buffer zone' between the three competing chiefdoms in the Valley of Oaxaca during the Rosario phase. It was built on a hill 400 metres above the valley floor,[10] with limited access to farmland and water,[11] which suggests other reasons for the founding of the settlement. One suggestion is that the location was intentionally chosen in the 'neutral' zone between the three competing chiefdoms as a political manoeuvre.[12]

Population and political organization

The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[13]
Although there is evidence for the presence of inherited social status and mortuary and residential elaboration during this period, the method of political organization is still unclear.[14] Archaeologist Arthur Joyce outlines possible power dynamics: 'The authority of the nobility may have been simultaneously couched in, but in dynamic tension with, traditional forms of authority that were more communal, egalitarian, and locally based.'[15]
There is a large range of population estimates for the valley during this period, from roughly 8000 to 15,000.[16][17] Monte Albán was the largest settlement in the Valley of Oaxaca during this period, although it was founded in a previously unoccupied location. The settlement began with around 2,000 people[18] and increased to over 5,000 towards the end of this period.[19] Much of the population of the valley moved to the newly founded settlement, which left sites such as San José Mogote abandoned.[20] Monte Albán became the largest settlement by far (324 ha in size towards the end of this period), but there were two other main settlements in the other arms of the valley (Yegüih and San Martin Tilcajete, the latter covering 52.8 ha by the end of the period).[21]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ 1,300 ♥ squared kilometers, based on the map given by Spencer (2009)[22]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [10,000-20,000] ♥ People.

Estimate for the area in and around Montel Alban

8,000-10,000: Although it is unlikely that all the settlements of the valley were unified (based on the divisions between populations of the different arms of the valley seen in the Rosario phase), the area in and around Monte Albán was the most densely populated. In the absence of a more accurate polity population estimate, the lower population estimate of 8,000-10,000 people given by Flannery and Marcus[23] is used as a very rough proxy for the Monte Albán polity population. 8,000-15,000: There is a large range of population estimates for the valley during this period, from roughly 8,000 to 15,000.[24][25]

Estimate for Monte Alban Early I sites in Valley of Oaxaca subareas

Monte Alban's population grew quickly to 5,000.[28]

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ 2,000: 500 BCE; 5,280: 400-200 BCE ♥ Inhabitants. Monte Albán was the largest settlement in the Valley of Oaxaca during this period, although it was founded in a previously unoccupied location. The settlement began with around 2,000 people[31], and increased to over 5,000 towards the end of this period.[32] 5,000: "From the area of the distribution of Early I sherds (and estimating a population of about 25-50 persons per hectare, less for the area of more scattered pottery), we estimate a population for Early I of 3,500-7,000 (Blanton 1978:33-35) and take the middle value of about 5,000 as the best estimate of population for the period. Population group continued into Late I, eventually reaching an estimated 17,000 (Blanton 1978:44)(fig. 3.4)."[33] Monte Alban's population grew quickly to 5,000.[34] "Table 11.3. Population in the largest centers, by phase, in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[35] Valley of Oaxaca population (Largest center in Oaxaca): Early I: 14652 (5250).[36]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [2-3] ♥ levels. There are different opinions as to how many levels of settlement hierarchy there were during this period. Much of the population of the valley moved to the newly founded settlement Monte Albán, which left sites such as San José Mogote abandoned.[37] Monte Albán became the largest settlement by far (324ha in size towards the end of this period), but there were two other main settlements in the other arms of the valley (Yegüih and San Martin Tilcajete, the latter covering 52.8ha by the end of this period)[38]. If these settlements are excluded (as they were the primary centres of different polities), then the remaining settlements in the Central and Etla valley areas may either form one or two levels, depending on the classification.[39][40] Yegüih (in Tlacolula subregion) and San Mart´ın Tilcajete (in Ocotl´an-Zimatl´an subregion) were primary centres in the other arms of the valley, with two further levels of settlement hierarchy.[41].

1. Monte Albán-primary centre, estimated at 324ha between 400-200 BCE, and with an 8km2 central complex.[42] (Yeguih in the Tlacolula subregion and San Mart´ın Tilcajete (52.8 ha) in the Ocotl´an-Zimatl´an subregion were also primary centres of different polities within the valley during this period.)[43]

2. villages-<2ha[44]

"Table 5.4. Monte Alban Early I population hierarchy in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[45]

Valley of Oaxaca: Level I: 5250; II: 578-1112; III: 210-301; IV: 107-179; No rank: 8-90.[46]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ suspected unknown ♥ [2-3] territorial levels but sources do not suggest there is evidence that they corresponded to administrative levels. Monte Albán was a primary center (given the size and central location of the settlement), with a series of regional second-order centres at the beginning of the Monte Albán I phase. There may also have been tertiary centres, if the smaller villages and hamlets are included.[47] The administrative levels have been coded as equivalent to the settlement hierarchy as there is little additional evidence for internal administration.

1. Monte Albán-primary centre, estimated at 324ha between 400-200 BCE, and with an 8km2 central complex.[48] (Yeguih in the Tlacolula subregion and San Mart´ın Tilcajete (52.8 ha) in the Ocotl´an-Zimatl´an subregion were also primary centres of different polities within the valley during this period.)[49]

2. villages-<2ha[50]

♠ Religious levels ♣ 1 ♥ Temples were present at this time but the standardised two-room temples was not yet present at Monte Alban, and so a permanent state religion of more than one organisational level cannot be inferred.[51]

♠ Military levels ♣ 1 ♥ level. Warriors were likely present during this period (based on the inter-polity conflict of this period and the preceding period) and so one level of military organisation is inferred, but sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time professional military personnel.[52]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucracy during this period. A degree of complex organisation is inferred to have existed, based on the construction of Monte Alban, but no written records or specialised administrative buildings have been identified.[56]

♠ Source of support ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucracy during this period. A degree of complex organisation is inferred to have existed, based on the construction of Monte Alban, but no written records or specialised administrative buildings have been identified.[57]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Evidence for Monte Alban architecture dating to this period is severely limited by later construction work from the MA II phase onwards.[65]

♠ Special purpose house ♣ absent ♥ The buildings during this period were primarily residential, and the specific function of non-residential buildings cannot be reliably identified until the architectural styles of the Monte Alban II phase appear.[66]

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ There is no direct evidence for a market system during this period (and no evidence for a market at the capital Monte Alban)[67], but other, indirect, evidence suggests a form of production and trade in the valley at this time. This evidence includes the specialisation of ceramic production by different communities (such as those seen in different assemblages at Monte Albán)[68], and the absence of evidence for state storage facilities, “a prominent byproduct of the redistributive economies, suggests the presence of a different mode of exchange.”.[69] We asked Gary Feinman[70] and he said: "Markets clearly have a long history in Mesoamerica before Aztec times. Back in the 1980s, I wrote a paper with Blanton and Kowalewski arguing that there were markets in Monte Albán I (ca. 500-200 BC). You can also find that argument in Ancient Mesoamerica and Ancient Oaxaca. While this may not yet be a consensual view yet, the literature on pre-Aztec markets across Mesoamerica is burgeoning."

♠ irrigation systems ♣ absent ♥ The use of small scale canal irrigation systems is inferred from the presence of settlements in the piedmont area (which would require some water control), but direct evidence for irrigation is found in the later periods. Any irrigation that was present would have been very small in extent and therefore not centrally controlled.[71][72][73]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ ♥ Probably unknown. Evidence for Monte Alban architecture dating to this period is severely limited by later construction work from the MA II phase onwards.[74]
♠ markets ♣ inferred present ♥ There is no direct evidence for a market system during this period (and no evidence for a market at the capital Monte Alban)[75], but other, indirect, evidence suggests a form of production and trade in the valley at this time. This evidence includes the specialisation of ceramic production by different communities (such as those seen in different assemblages at Monte Albán)[76], and the absence of evidence for state storage facilities, “a prominent byproduct of the redistributive economies, suggests the presence of a different mode of exchange.”.[77] We asked Gary Feinman[78] and he said: "Markets clearly have a long history in Mesoamerica before Aztec times. Back in the 1980s, I wrote a paper with Blanton and Kowalewski arguing that there were markets in Monte Albán I (ca. 500-200 BC). You can also find that argument in Ancient Mesoamerica and Ancient Oaxaca. While this may not yet be a consensual view yet, the literature on pre-Aztec markets across Mesoamerica is burgeoning."
♠ food storage sites ♣ absent ♥ No evidence for centralised food storage has been found at Monte Alban.[79][80]

♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥ Although many buildings from Monte Albán have been destroyed or covered by later construction, some structures dating from this time have survived and likely had symbolic functions: the Patio south of Mound A (or PSA) (which consisted of sloping stone wall in deep pit in the North Platform); the Edificio de los Danzantes (which was a platform mound with a gallery of 300 carvings of slain captives (similar to earlier one at San Jose Mogote); and Stelae 12 and 13 (which had calendric and noncalendric hieroglyphs carved into the surface).[81]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ absent ♥ The buildings during this period were primarily residential, and the specific function of non-residential buildings cannot be reliably identified until the architectural styles of the Monte Alban II phase appear.[82]

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ absent ♥ The buildings during this period were primarily residential, and the specific function of non-residential buildings cannot be reliably identified until the architectural styles of the Monte Alban II phase appear.[83]

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ absent ♥ Small roads were constructed through Monte Alban, but sources do not suggest there is evidence for a road network linking settlements.[84] We asked Gary Feinman about roads in Oaxacan polities and he said: "It depends on what you mean by roads. There are definite roads/accessways within sites. Blanton defines some at Monte Albán and Linda [Nicholas] and I defined some at El Palmillo. These likely were not paved, but they may have been banked and were cleared. Between sites there are known 16th century trails, which were likely used for a long, long time. Again, they likely were not paved, but there were no beasts of burden."[85] Coded absent: we do not count accessways within settlements or paths and trails not constructed deliberately as roads.
♠ Bridges ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for the construction of bridges during this period.[86]
♠ Canals ♣ absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for the construction of canals during this period.[87]
♠ Ports ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.

The most impressive/costly building(s)[edit]

♠ height ♣ ♥
♠ extent ♣ ♥
♠ cost ♣ ♥ 800-200 BCE. Structures face with large stones carved in relief: Danzantes; Mound J (thought to be astronomical observatory); urban layout in Montenegro (streets, plazas, stepped temples) .[88] Classic Period 600-300 BCE. Complex M. Ball court. Great Plaza bounded to north and south by immense platforms. North platform 200m length. Ceremonial center constructed on mountain top overlooking three valleys at altitude 400m. City on slopes below.[89] The ceremonial center "appears as one harmonius unit, laid out around a plaza 1,000 feet (320m) from north to south and 40 feet (140m) from east to west)".[90] "Danzantes Gallery, a platform whose plaza-facing walls consisted of rows of carved stone slabs". "Another Period I structure that was partially exposed along the west edge of the plaza features a 6-meter-high wall constructed of large stones and rubble masonry columns."[91] "Santo Tomas Mazaltepec, situated at the western edge of the Etla arm of the valley, had a group of three mounts enclosing a large plaza measuring 49 by 35 meters."[92]

Information[edit]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ The Zapotec unit of yaguen (26-27cm, or the length of a forearm) was used as a measure, and many stone monuments at Monte Alban are multiples of a yaguen.[100]
♠ Area ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[101]
♠ Volume ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[102]
♠ Weight ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[103]
♠ Time ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[104]
♠ Geometrical ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[105]
♠ Other ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[106]

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[107]
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ Roughly 350 inscribed stones have been found at Monte Albán (including 310 danzantes) assigned to MA I and II.[108] Mesoamerican civilizations, including the Maya, Aztec, Mixtec and Zapotec all possessed "a true form of writing: a series of hieroglyphs arranged in vertical columns and in many instances combined with numerals. The glyphs were at least indirectly related to a spoken language." Zapotec inscriptions are considered true writing, since the inscriptions had verbs.[109]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Evidence for carved glyphs (of names and calendar dates) has been found.[110]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ present ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[111]
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[112]

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[113]
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ Several glyphs carved on stones at Monte Albán have been interpreted as calendrical glyphs, based on analogy with later periods[114][115] More specifically, the glyphs on Stele 12 and 13 at Monte Alban seem to refer to days on the 365 day calendar, or yza secular year as it was known in the historical periods. In addition to evidence for the ritual 260 day calendar (or piye calendar) at San Jose Mogote, this suggests that both the two calendars were being used from this time.[116]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[117]
♠ Religious literature ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[118]
♠ Practical literature ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[119]
♠ History ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[120]
♠ Philosophy ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[121]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[122]
♠ Fiction ♣ absent ♥ Glyphs dating to this period have been deciphered as either calendrical dates or the names of prisoners. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[123]

Military Technologies[edit]

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[136][137]
♠ Bronze ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[138][139]
♠ Iron ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[140][141]
♠ Steel ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[142][143]

Projectiles[edit]

♠ Javelins ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[144] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include javelins. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.

♠ Atlatl ♣ present ♥ Present in the valley of Oaxaca since preceramic times (the Proto-Otomangueans) for hunting.[146] and, in previous periods, obsidian blades were found in Tomb 10 at San José Mogote which may have been hafted into atlatl darts.[147] In addition, glyphs depicting what may be atlatls or spearthrowers have been carved with the danzantes at San Jose Mogote.[148]
♠ Slings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[149] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include slings. Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ present ♥ Monte Albán was built on a hill 400m above the valley floor and a number of other settlements were located on hilltops.[178][179]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Monte Albán was built with a 3km defensive wall along the shallower slopes of the hill, and wooden palisades may have been present.[180]
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ present ♥ The defensive wall around Monte Alban was made of earth and stone.[181][182]
♠ Ditch ♣ inferred absent ♥ Monte Albán's fortifications are relatively well understood, but no source mentions the existence of a ditch.[183]
♠ Moat ♣ inferred absent ♥ Monte Albán's fortifications are relatively well understood, but no source mentions the existence of a moat.[184]
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ present ♥ The defensive wall around Monte Alban was made of earth and stone.[185][186]
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ absent ♥ The defensive wall around Monte Alban was made of earth and stone.[187][188]
♠ Fortified camps ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Complex fortifications ♣ absent ♥ Monte Albán was built with a 3km defensive wall along the shallower slopes of the hill.[189] Another wall was constructed along the northern boundary of Monte Albán, but not until the Late I or II periods.[190]
♠ Long walls ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Modern fortifications ♣ absent ♥ Gunpowder not yet invented.

List of Wars[edit]

Only inter-chiefdom raiding is known from this period[227], and specific battles cannot be identified in the archaeological record.

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Rosalind Purcell; Enrico Cioni ♥ RP responsible for original coding of this section; EC supplemented and replaced RP's data with data from more recent and comprehensive sources.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥ The name of the historical or archaeological expert who supervised coding and checked/improved/approved the result. If more than one expert was involved, list all.

Description of the Ritual System

Rituals are described below using archaeological variables due to lack of evidence for discreet rituals.


Rites of passage

Death Burials-Present-fewer than ten tombs found from this period-they are rectangular, roofed with stone slabs and no door. Many more non-tomb burials have also been found. [228]

Grave goods-Present-the poorest graves had pottery, the richest had many grave goods including conch shell effigies, duck and frog effigies [229]

Gatherings Ritual Space Architecture Present-in Monte Alban three public buildings, isolated in non-residential area, potentially neutral and sacred area [230] Partially enclosed-two sides closed, two sides open E-W, with stairs leading into building on both sides. Suggested uses include one side for priests, one for public; or ritual in which participants entered the temple from one side and exited the other. [231]

At Monte Negro four public buildings described as temples have been found. They vary in shape. Temple T had a 2m high platform. Many temples had columns and contained tombs. Size-Temple T 8.8 x 6.4m, enclosed At Monte Negro elite houses have secret tunnels to the temples suggesting that access to the supernatural beings of the temples was differential

Plaza-At Tomaltepec, public buildings flank a rectangular plaza 50m on a side. [232]

Symbolic Carvings-Present-Monte Alban one public building on the North Platform had a tablero covered with stucco serpentine motifs. Carvings on Temple 2 are the same motifs used to represent blood drops at San Jose Mogote. [233]

Monuments-Present-Over 300 carved stone monuments known as danzantes. Taller than they are wide they are carved with pictures of individuals in different poses. There is some conjecture as to what the individuals are doing; ideas include dancing, swimming, ecstatic priests. Flannery and Marcus agree with Coe’s idea that they represent “the corpses of captives slain or sacrificed by the rulers of Monte Alban. The figures carved on the stairway risers are analogous to those on prisoner staircases in the Maya region, where anyone ascending the stairway would tread on their bodies.” [234] Carvings show sexually mutilated corpse. [235]


Feasting Unknown

Anthropomorphic Representations Present-effigy bottles found in grave goods and elsewhere, thought to represent ancestors and gods. Effigy braziers found at Monte Negro. [236]

Ritual objects Present -2 obsidian sacrificial daggers were found in Temple T-south in Monte Negro, they are the same type as those used in later periods to remove the hearts of human sacrificial victims, it is concluded that human sacrifice occurred. [237]


Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ public sacrifice rituals ♥ "The Zapotec view of the cosmos was therefore materially inscribed on the art and architecture of the Main Plaza with references to sacrifice, warfare, and the underworld to the south and the celestial realm to the north. If I am correct in my interpretation of the Main Plaza, then rituals like human sacrifice and ancestor veneration, which invoked the cosmic creation, were performed amid a material symbol of the Zapotec cosmos. The scale, accessibility, openness, and symbolism of the Main Plaza indicate that it was constructed as an arena where thousands of people could have participated in public rituals. Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below. Crowds could have entered the plaza from the east, moving from the everyday world of their homes below into a sacred space materializing symbolic references to the sacred past of ancestors, deities, and the cosmic creation. Ceremonies included a blending of traditional activities such as autosacrificial bloodletting, ancestor veneration, divination, and ritual feasting with new practices like human sacrifice (Blanton et al. 1999:105- 7; A. Joyce 2000, 2004; Orr 1997, 2001; Urcid 2008). The images on the Building L-sub and Building J programs could be viewed as processions moved past and would have continually communicated the significance of sacrifice and warfare. Sacrificial practices, especially human sacrifice, were particularly significant in contacting deities and ancestors, reenacting the cosmic creation and the sacred covenant, and renewing the world (A. Joyce 2000; Monaghan 1990). Human sacrifice now linked the activation of the sacred covenant to warfare. Enemies would have been redefined not just as competitors, but also in sacred terms as potential sacrificial victims." [238]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [2000-5000] ♥ "The scale, accessibility, openness, and symbolism of the Main Plaza indicate that it was constructed as an arena where thousands of people could have participated in public rituals." [239]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "Recent iconographic interpretations suggest that specialized ritual abilities may have been the prerogative not just of nobles, but also of high-ranking commoners who achieved positions of ritual authority (Urcid 2008; Urcid & Winter 2003)." [240] However, "Human sacrifice[...] was a new ritual practice controlled by elites since only nobles would have had the resources and power to sponsor raids, take captives, and organize public ceremonies. [...] The founding of Monte Albán, the construction of the Main Plaza, public rituals, and warfare were activities that were almost certainly organized and led by nobles." [241]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below." [242] "The size, accessibility, and symbolism of the Main Plaza suggest to me that during Monte Albán’s first four centuries the plaza was a focus of public ceremonies participated in by people of varied identities including different statuses, corporate groups, and communities." [243]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ "Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below." Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ women's rituals ♥ "Household rituals continued to be dominated by small, modeled figurines similar to those of earlier periods (Martínez López & Winter 1994), suggesting that the domestic rituals of common people had not changed significantly with the founding of Monte Albán." [260] NOTE: Women's rituals are here considered "most widespread" because, unlike other rituals, which were confined to specialised buildings, these ones were performed in virtually every house. Moreover, figurines have been found in Monte Alban's hinterland, not just in Monte Alban itself [261]. However, Marcus reconstructs two possible women's rituals, and only one can be coded. The divination ritual has been chosen because it is very slightly better understood. However, relevant passages describing the figurine ritual have been included here because it might be useful. "Although there is no sign of women’s tools or activities near our early public buildings, we do find evidence for women’s ritual in three places: the house (yoho quixi or yoho coba); the associated ramada or lean-to (yoho yaha or yoho yaa); and the surrounding dooryard. These activities are believed to include (1) divination by the casting of beans or maize kernels, and (2) communication with recent ancestors through the use of small solid figurines. "Let us begin with divination, which—by analogy with ethnohistoric descriptions—may have been carried out in special basins in the dooryard of the house. Such basins were waterproofed with a layer of lime plaster, then painted in one of the colors associated with the four Zapotec world directions: red, yellow, white, or black. Two such painted basins were discovered in the yard of a San José phase house, Household C3, at San José Mogote (Fig. 9). East of the house was a red-painted basin, while a yellow-painted basin lay 3 m to the south. The red-painted basin was 1.2 m in diameter and recessed 5 cm into the ground; it had been mud-plastered, given a waterproof coating of lime, then painted red with specular hematite. The yellow-painted basin was similar in every way except color. Whether there were once four basins—each painted a different color and oriented to one of the four world directions—is not known, since exposing the relevant areas of the yard would have required the destruction of a later building (Marcus 1989: fig. 8.20). "We know from sixteenth-century documents that Zapotec diviners sat on a mat and cast maize kernels onto the surface of a water-filled basin, noting whether they fell or floated in groups of 2, 3, 5, or 13. This use of water (niça) for casting lots was called tiniyaaya niça, and it was used to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child. We suspect that the painted basins of Household C3 may have served for such divination (Fig. 10). "Zapotec women also petitioned their recently deceased ancestors for guidance, often doing so in or near the house where that ancestor had lived. We suspect, on the basis of ethnographic analogy, that small solid figurines— of which we have found hundreds in household context—provided the medium to which the spirits of specific ancestors could return during this petitioning. Women preferred to petition their female ancestors, which may account for the fact that the majority of these figurines are women." [262] "Women’s ritual seems to have been conducted in the house, the adjacent lean-to, and the surrounding dooryard. The recently deceased ancestors—both males and females, but especially females—appear to have been the focus of much of this ritual. Small solid figurines, sometimes arranged in ritual scenes, evidently provided a venue to which the spirits of the ancestors could return; in that venue they were probably consulted, petitioned, and involved in the ongoing affairs of their descendants. Women also made figurines of animals (principally dogs and birds), which may have served the ancestors as “animal agents” like those described for China." [263] "In later periods in China, animals continued to be important as lineage emblems, playing roles in ritual and in communicating with the Sky, Earth, and the dead (Chang 1980: 209).The Shang-period Chinese (1200-1000 b.c.) depicted animals on bronze vessels, including dragons or snakes. Such depictions were called wu, a term that has been translated “animal offerings.”According to K. C. Chang (1983: 65): 'to make animal offerings was a concrete means of achieving communication between heaven and earth, the dead and the living. We thus come to the inevitable conclusion that the animal designs on Shang and Chou bronzes are iconographically meaningful: they are images of the various animals that served as the helpers of shamans and shamanesses in the task of communication between heaven and earth, the spirits and the living.'" [264]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ monthly ♥ "Most likely, this ritual was performed simply "to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child." [265]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ monthly ♥ "Most likely, this ritual was performed simply "to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child." [266]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ monthly ♥ "Most likely, this ritual was performed simply "to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child." [267]
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all women. "There is no reason to believe any women in the village were excluded from any of the rituals discussed, since they conducted them in their own households." [268]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ all women. "There is no reason to believe any women in the village were excluded from any of the rituals discussed, since they conducted them in their own households." [269]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "There is no reason to believe any women in the village were excluded from any of the rituals discussed, since they conducted them in their own households." [270]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ women's rituals ♥ "Household rituals continued to be dominated by small, modeled figurines similar to those of earlier periods (Martínez López & Winter 1994), suggesting that the domestic rituals of common people had not changed significantly with the founding of Monte Albán." [290] NOTE: Though Marcus reconstructs two possible women's rituals, only one can be coded. The divination ritual has been chosen because it is very slightly better understood. However, relevant passages describing the figurine ritual have been included here because it might be useful. "Although there is no sign of women’s tools or activities near our early public buildings, we do find evidence for women’s ritual in three places: the house (yoho quixi or yoho coba); the associated ramada or lean-to (yoho yaha or yoho yaa); and the surrounding dooryard. These activities are believed to include (1) divination by the casting of beans or maize kernels, and (2) communication with recent ancestors through the use of small solid figurines. "Let us begin with divination, which—by analogy with ethnohistoric descriptions—may have been carried out in special basins in the dooryard of the house. Such basins were waterproofed with a layer of lime plaster, then painted in one of the colors associated with the four Zapotec world directions: red, yellow, white, or black. Two such painted basins were discovered in the yard of a San José phase house, Household C3, at San José Mogote (Fig. 9). East of the house was a red-painted basin, while a yellow-painted basin lay 3 m to the south. The red-painted basin was 1.2 m in diameter and recessed 5 cm into the ground; it had been mud-plastered, given a waterproof coating of lime, then painted red with specular hematite. The yellow-painted basin was similar in every way except color. Whether there were once four basins—each painted a different color and oriented to one of the four world directions—is not known, since exposing the relevant areas of the yard would have required the destruction of a later building (Marcus 1989: fig. 8.20). "We know from sixteenth-century documents that Zapotec diviners sat on a mat and cast maize kernels onto the surface of a water-filled basin, noting whether they fell or floated in groups of 2, 3, 5, or 13. This use of water (niça) for casting lots was called tiniyaaya niça, and it was used to determine the cause and cure of illnesses affecting household members; to predict whether a particular day was auspicious for planting or marriage; or to assign a name to a newborn child. We suspect that the painted basins of Household C3 may have served for such divination (Fig. 10). "Zapotec women also petitioned their recently deceased ancestors for guidance, often doing so in or near the house where that ancestor had lived. We suspect, on the basis of ethnographic analogy, that small solid figurines— of which we have found hundreds in household context—provided the medium to which the spirits of specific ancestors could return during this petitioning. Women preferred to petition their female ancestors, which may account for the fact that the majority of these figurines are women." [291] "Women’s ritual seems to have been conducted in the house, the adjacent lean-to, and the surrounding dooryard. The recently deceased ancestors—both males and females, but especially females—appear to have been the focus of much of this ritual. Small solid figurines, sometimes arranged in ritual scenes, evidently provided a venue to which the spirits of the ancestors could return; in that venue they were probably consulted, petitioned, and involved in the ongoing affairs of their descendants. Women also made figurines of animals (principally dogs and birds), which may have served the ancestors as “animal agents” like those described for China." [292] "In later periods in China, animals continued to be important as lineage emblems, playing roles in ritual and in communicating with the Sky, Earth, and the dead (Chang 1980: 209).The Shang-period Chinese (1200-1000 b.c.) depicted animals on bronze vessels, including dragons or snakes. Such depictions were called wu, a term that has been translated “animal offerings.”According to K. C. Chang (1983: 65): 'to make animal offerings was a concrete means of achieving communication between heaven and earth, the dead and the living. We thus come to the inevitable conclusion that the animal designs on Shang and Chou bronzes are iconographically meaningful: they are images of the various animals that served as the helpers of shamans and shamanesses in the task of communication between heaven and earth, the spirits and the living.'" [293]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ public sacrifice rituals ♥ "The Zapotec view of the cosmos was therefore materially inscribed on the art and architecture of the Main Plaza with references to sacrifice, warfare, and the underworld to the south and the celestial realm to the north. If I am correct in my interpretation of the Main Plaza, then rituals like human sacrifice and ancestor veneration, which invoked the cosmic creation, were performed amid a material symbol of the Zapotec cosmos. The scale, accessibility, openness, and symbolism of the Main Plaza indicate that it was constructed as an arena where thousands of people could have participated in public rituals. Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below. Crowds could have entered the plaza from the east, moving from the everyday world of their homes below into a sacred space materializing symbolic references to the sacred past of ancestors, deities, and the cosmic creation. Ceremonies included a blending of traditional activities such as autosacrificial bloodletting, ancestor veneration, divination, and ritual feasting with new practices like human sacrifice (Blanton et al. 1999:105- 7; A. Joyce 2000, 2004; Orr 1997, 2001; Urcid 2008). The images on the Building L-sub and Building J programs could be viewed as processions moved past and would have continually communicated the significance of sacrifice and warfare. Sacrificial practices, especially human sacrifice, were particularly significant in contacting deities and ancestors, reenacting the cosmic creation and the sacred covenant, and renewing the world (A. Joyce 2000; Monaghan 1990). Human sacrifice now linked the activation of the sacred covenant to warfare. Enemies would have been redefined not just as competitors, but also in sacred terms as potential sacrificial victims." [319]

♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [2000-5000] ♥ "The scale, accessibility, openness, and symbolism of the Main Plaza indicate that it was constructed as an arena where thousands of people could have participated in public rituals." [320]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "Recent iconographic interpretations suggest that specialized ritual abilities may have been the prerogative not just of nobles, but also of high-ranking commoners who achieved positions of ritual authority (Urcid 2008; Urcid & Winter 2003)." [321] However, "Human sacrifice[...] was a new ritual practice controlled by elites since only nobles would have had the resources and power to sponsor raids, take captives, and organize public ceremonies. [...] The founding of Monte Albán, the construction of the Main Plaza, public rituals, and warfare were activities that were almost certainly organized and led by nobles." [322]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below." [323] "The size, accessibility, and symbolism of the Main Plaza suggest to me that during Monte Albán’s first four centuries the plaza was a focus of public ceremonies participated in by people of varied identities including different statuses, corporate groups, and communities." [324]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Unlike the preceding rituals, the most dysphoric collective ritual may not belong to the official cult, but may be practiced by another group that has a significant role in the polity.

♠ Name ♣ ♥ In the narrative part, also give a brief description of the ritual
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of the ritual specialist (e.g. priest).
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of the participants. This group of participants does not include the ritual specialists, like priests. Highly engaged audiences (audiences who are processing, jeering, ...) are considered as participants. Audiences consisting of passive spectators or witnesses are coded for in the next variable. When it is difficult to distinguish between a highly engaged audience and an audience consisting of passive spectators, code these audiences here.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of a typical member of an audience consisting of passive spectators or witnesses.

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an elite, the nature of leadership is still unclear as is the relationship between elites and the military. "...it is difficult to identify particular rulers or to understand how power was exercised."[341] From Religion: "Although hereditary nobles are increasingly evident in the archaeological record, representations of rulers are muted in public expressions of political and religious authority. The authority of the nobility may have been simultaneously couched in, but in dynamic tension with, traditional forms of authority that were more communal, egalitarian, and locally based. Residential and mortuary data, however, indicate increasing social differentiation in the Oaxaca Valley during the Late/Terminal Formative (Barber & Joyce 2006:223- 9; Martínez López et al. 1995:236- 38; Winter 1986:341- 2)." [342] The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[343] Warriors were likely present during this period (based on the inter-polity conflict of this period and the preceding period), but sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time professional military personnel.[344]It has been suggested that the polity centred at Monte Alban was a confederacy of people from different areas of the valley, and was not large or powerful enough to constitute a state or support an army.[345] Conflict between the chiefdoms occupying the Valley of Oaxaca was present during this period.[346]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an elite the nature of leadership is still unclear as is the relationship between elites and possible administrators. "...it is difficult to identify particular rulers or to understand how power was exercised."[347] From Religion: "Although hereditary nobles are increasingly evident in the archaeological record, representations of rulers are muted in public expressions of political and religious authority. The authority of the nobility may have been simultaneously couched in, but in dynamic tension with, traditional forms of authority that were more communal, egalitarian, and locally based. Residential and mortuary data, however, indicate increasing social differentiation in the Oaxaca Valley during the Late/Terminal Formative (Barber & Joyce 2006:223- 9; Martínez López et al. 1995:236- 38; Winter 1986:341- 2)." [348] The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[349] It has been suggested that the polity centred at Monte Alban was a confederacy of people from different areas of the valley, and was not large or powerful enough to constitute a state or support an army.[350] Conflict between the chiefdoms occupying the Valley of Oaxaca was present during this period.[351]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an elite the nature of leadership is still unclear, as is the relationship between elites and possible administrators. "...it is difficult to identify particular rulers or to understand how power was exercised."[352] From Religion: "Although hereditary nobles are increasingly evident in the archaeological record, representations of rulers are muted in public expressions of political and religious authority. The authority of the nobility may have been simultaneously couched in, but in dynamic tension with, traditional forms of authority that were more communal, egalitarian, and locally based. Residential and mortuary data, however, indicate increasing social differentiation in the Oaxaca Valley during the Late/Terminal Formative (Barber & Joyce 2006:223- 9; Martínez López et al. 1995:236- 38; Winter 1986:341- 2)." [353] The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[354] It has been suggested that the polity centred at Monte Alban was a confederacy of people from different areas of the valley, and was not large or powerful enough to constitute a state or support an army.[355] Conflict between the chiefdoms occupying the Valley of Oaxaca was present during this period.[356]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of an elite the nature of leadership is still unclear, as is the relationship between elites and possible administrators. "...it is difficult to identify particular rulers or to understand how power was exercised."[357] The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[358] Sources do not suggest there is evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[359] Evidence for writing other than carved glyphs (of names and calendar dates) has not been found.[360] Roughly 350 inscribed stones have been found at Monte Albán (including 310 danzantes) assigned to MA I and II.[361]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ inferred absent ♥ "...it is difficult to identify particular rulers or to understand how power was exercised."[453] Sources do not suggest there is evidence for full-time bureaucracy during this period. ♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ suspected unknown ♥ There is no evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[573] Evidence for writing other than carved glyphs (of names and calendar dates) has not been found.[574] Roughly 350 inscribed stones have been found at Monte Albán (including 310 danzantes) assigned to MA I and II.[575] Monte Albán was founded at the beginning of this period and located in what was previously a “buffer zone” between the three competing chiefdoms in the valley during the Rosario phase. It was built on a hill 400m above the valley floor[576] and with limited access to farmland and water[577] which suggests other reasons for the founding of the settlement. One suggestion is that the location was intentionally chosen in the “neutral” zone between the three competing chiefdoms as a political manoeuvre.[578] Monte Albán grew rapidly to an estimated population of 5,000 people at the beginning of this period, partly from the abandonment of other settlements and immigration of those populations to Monte Albán.[579]

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ From Religion: "Although hereditary nobles are increasingly evident in the archaeological record, representations of rulers are muted in public expressions of political and religious authority. The authority of the nobility may have been simultaneously couched in, but in dynamic tension with, traditional forms of authority that were more communal, egalitarian, and locally based. Residential and mortuary data, however, indicate increasing social differentiation in the Oaxaca Valley during the Late/Terminal Formative (Barber & Joyce 2006:223- 9; Martínez López et al. 1995:236- 38; Winter 1986:341- 2)." [610] "Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below." [611] "The size, accessibility, and symbolism of the Main Plaza suggest to me that during Monte Albán’s first four centuries the plaza was a focus of public ceremonies participated in by people of varied identities including different statuses, corporate groups, and communities." [612] From Ritual: "Recent iconographic interpretations suggest that specialized ritual abilities may have been the prerogative not just of nobles, but also of high-ranking commoners who achieved positions of ritual authority (Urcid 2008; Urcid & Winter 2003)." [613] However, "Human sacrifice[...] was a new ritual practice controlled by elites since only nobles would have had the resources and power to sponsor raids, take captives, and organize public ceremonies. [...] The founding of Monte Albán, the construction of the Main Plaza, public rituals, and warfare were activities that were almost certainly organized and led by nobles." [614]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ present ♥ Only raiding between chiefdoms is known from this period. Captives were taken but depicted as dead and mutilated, rather than as slaves.[635] Monuments - Present - Over 300 carved stone monuments known as danzantes. Taller than they are wide they are carved with pictures of individuals in different poses. There is some conjecture as to what the individuals are doing; ideas include dancing, swimming, ecstatic priests. Flannery and Marcus agree with Coe’s idea that they represent “the corpses of captives slain or sacrificed by the rulers of Monte Alban. The figures carved on the stairway risers are analogous to those on prisoner staircases in the Maya region, where anyone ascending the stairway would tread on their bodies.” [636] Carvings show sexually mutilated corpse. [637] Human sacrifice now linked the activation of the sacred covenant to warfare. Enemies would have been redefined not just as competitors, but also in sacred terms as potential sacrificial victims." [638]
♠ Low social class ♣ inferred absent ♥ From Ritual: Emphasis appears to be on sacrifice of enemy captives rather than in-group members, at least in these rituals [639].
♠ Elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ From Ritual: Emphasis appears to be on sacrifice of enemy captives rather than in-group members, at least in these rituals [640].
♠ Retainers ♣ inferred absent ♥ From Ritual: Emphasis appears to be on sacrifice of enemy captives rather than in-group members, at least in these rituals [641].
♠ Outsiders ♣ present ♥ From Ritual: Human sacrifice now linked the activation of the sacred covenant to warfare. Enemies would have been redefined not just as competitors, but also in sacred terms as potential sacrificial victims." [642]

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

We are interested here in any systems of thought and behavior that can influence people's actions, which we term a Normative Ideology. Normative ideologies are thought-systems concerned with the correct behavior of people, governments/leaders, and other groups (and particularly the relationships between these groups).

Mainly, this will be a religious or ritual system. As usual, when we mention Religious or Ritual System our focus is on the 'official cult', defined the same way as in the Rituals section:

With the official cult we refer to the set of collective religious practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

However, Normative Ideologies are not restricted to religious/ritual systems. They include other thought systems, such as philosophy or anything that prescribes a particular pattern of behaviour. An example is classical Greek philosophy, such as the works of Plato and Aristotle, who were concerned with correct or moral behaviour and whose thoughts influenced the actual practice of several societies (the empire of Alexander the Great, notably).

Deification of Rulers[edit]

("gods"is a shorthand for "supernatural agents’)

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ inferred present ♥ "The role of nobles as ritual specialists, especially sacrificers, dramatically communicated and reinforced their identities as mediators between commoners and the sacred." [643] However, the following is also worth noting: "If the number of vessels interred as burial offerings is related to a person’s commensal networks and perhaps to the number of people participating in the mortuary ceremony, then both nobles and influential commoners had extensive social ties. Effigy vessels and urns, sometimes depicting Cocijo, and a variety of zoomorphic vessels usually representing animals associated with water including ducks, conch shells, frogs, and toads are much more common in tombs and burials with elaborate offerings. These data suggest that associations with rain and the rain god were related to status, both achieved and ascribed by birth. Recent iconographic interpretations suggest that specialized ritual abilities may have been the prerogative not just of nobles, but also of high-ranking commoners who achieved positions of ritual authority (Urcid 2008; Urcid & Winter 2003)." [644]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ present ♥ "Royal ancestor worship was highly developed among the Zapotec, and because so much of their prehistory is known, we can see its components forming far back in time (Flannery and Marcus 1976, 1983b; Marcus 1978, 1989a). As long ago as 1150-850 B.C. one can see basic elements such as 'earth' and 'sky' depicted in stylized form on Oaxacan funerary ceramics (Marcus 1989a). 'Sky' was often represented by Cociyo, 'Lightning,' the most powerful and revered force in the Zapotec cosmos." [645]

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred present ♥ "Royal ancestor worship was highly developed among the Zapotec, and because so much of their prehistory is known, we can see its components forming far back in time (Flannery and Marcus 1976, 1983b; Marcus 1978, 1989a). Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

These codes refer to acts undertaken without direct compulsion from or out of adherence to a religious system (religious aspects of prosociality are coded below)

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ [present; absent] ♥ "Although hereditary nobles are increasingly evident in the archaeological record, representations of rulers are muted in public expressions of political and religious authority. The authority of the nobility may have been simultaneously couched in, but in dynamic tension with, traditional forms of authority that were more communal, egalitarian, and locally based. Residential and mortuary data, however, indicate increasing social differentiation in the Oaxaca Valley during the Late/Terminal Formative (Barber & Joyce 2006:223- 9; Martínez López et al. 1995:236- 38; Winter 1986:341- 2)." [647] "Despite increasing political and religious authority, public settings like the Main Plaza stressed the symbols of communal authority and an emerging corporate identity, while muting representations of the increasingly powerful rulers of the city. Although nobles lived near the ceremonial precinct and directed public rituals, until the Classic period (ad 300- 800) the Main Plaza itself had few overt representations of local nobles and there were no high-status residences directly facing the plaza. Rulers were represented solely in the hieroglyphic inscriptions set in Building L-sub, which were probably understandable only to the literate nobility. The earliest known ruler’s portrait, Monument J-41, dates to c.ad 100. [...] Although the Main Plaza was a public space focused on cosmic symbolism and community, ritual practices carried out there also contributed to the power of the nobility and an increasing separation of noble and commoner identities (A. Joyce 2000; Urcid 2008). Based on the iconographic and epigraphic evidence, as well as analogies with the early colonial period, public ceremonies were probably organized and led by nobles, and perhaps high-ranking members of religious and military organizations. The role of nobles as ritual specialists, especially sacrificers, dramatically communicated and reinforced their identities as mediators between commoners and the sacred." [648]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

Human sacrifice (HS) is defined as deliberate and ritualized killing of an individual to please or placate supernatural beings. Common examples of supernatural beings are gods and ancestors, including a recently deceased one. In other words, sacrificing humans to follow a high-status individual in the afterlife is a legitimate example of HS. HS is distinct from capital punishment, however, even for offenses against religion (e.g., burning of heretics, punishment of vestals for transgressions). Such practices should be coded as Capital Punishment for Religious Offenses. Execution of 'witches' even if regularly occurring at funerals of a high-status individual is not HS. We can reflect such practices under Capital Punishment for Witchcraft. See Law, Robin. 1985. Human Sacrifice in Pre-Colonial West Africa. African Affairs 84 (334):53-87 for further discussion.

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [2-5] ♥ "Sacrificial practices, especially human sacrifice, were particularly significant in contacting deities and ancestors, reenacting the cosmic creation and the sacred covenant, and renewing the world (A. Joyce 2000; Monaghan 1990). Human sacrifice now linked the activation of the sacred covenant to warfare. Enemies would have been redefined not just as competitors, but also in sacred terms as potential sacrificial victims." [660]

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ human sacrifice of an in-group member ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ human sacrifice of a low-status individual ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ human sacrifice of a criminal ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ human sacrifice of a high status individual ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ human sacrifice of a relative ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ human sacrifice of other ♣ present ♥ "Sacrificial practices, especially human sacrifice, were particularly significant in contacting deities and ancestors, reenacting the cosmic creation and the sacred covenant, and renewing the world (A. Joyce 2000; Monaghan 1990). Human sacrifice now linked the activation of the sacred covenant to warfare. Enemies would have been redefined not just as competitors, but also in sacred terms as potential sacrificial victims." [661]

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).

♠ respect for authority ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Participation in public ceremonies created powerful memories that bound people to the rulers, the symbols, and the new social order centered at Monte Albán. [...] Participation in public ritual performances contributed to the production of larger-scale corporate identities internalized in people’s dispositions and externalized in social practices like contributing tribute, allegiance, and labor to rulers." [669] However, no evidence could be found that this value was actually enforced. And even if respect for authority was enforced--which does seem likely--it's not obvious that this would have constituted enforcement of morality, as opposed to, for example, law enforcement, or, indeed, simple enforcement of the hierarchical order, with no connection to morality proper.

[670] However, no evidence could be found that bravery was actually enforced.

♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ absent ♥ Monetary items have not been found dating to this period.[684]
♠ Public markets ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "We also have no evidence that any production took place in nondomestic, state-supervised workshops; rather craft manufacture appears to have remained in residential contexts. Instead of a redistributive economy, we postulate that much Period I exchange occurred in marketplaces frequented by household producers and consumers (Blanton 1983; Blanton, Kowalewski, Feinman, and Appel 1982:65-68; Feinman, Blanton, and Kowalewski 1984). If production was primarily in the hands of households, it is hard to see how distribution or exchange could have been controlled by a central political institution. Market exchange and marketplaces certainly were important when the Spanish first described sixteenth-century Oaxaca, and they continue to be important institutions to this day (Beals 1975; Cook and Diskin 1976). It seems reasonable to suggest that these thriving contact-period institutions had a long prior history, even though it is difficult to identify market exchange or even marketplaces archaeologically. We still lack indisputable evidence for this hypothesis. Our surveys did find at least one site that had features consistent with a marketplace - a low hill in the middle of the Valle Grande area, south of Monte Albán, not in one of the district capitals or other civic-ceremonial centers, but between them, at the intersection of three proposed district boundaries. The site dates to Early and Late I. It has no mounds; instead it has a large (55 by 38 meters), open, easily accessible platform bordered by large boulders. There is an unusual amount of artifactual evidence here for specialized production: misfired pottery, dense concentrations of chipped chert and quartzite, a tunnel into bedrock apparently to mine quartz, two circular bedrock features that may have served in a grinding or pounding operation, and a bark beater for making paper (Blanton, Kowalewski, Feinman, and Appel 1982:262). The location, open plaza, and artifactual evidence are what one might expect for a market."[685] There is no direct evidence for a market system during this period (and no evidence for a market at the capital Monte Alban)[686], but other, indirect, evidence suggests a form of production and trade in the valley at this time. This evidence includes the specialisation of ceramic production by different communities (such as those seen in different assemblages at Monte Albán)[687], and the absence of evidence for state storage facilities, “a prominent byproduct of the redistributive economies, suggests the presence of a different mode of exchange.”.[688]

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Skeletal analyses show that the city's Period I inhabitants were reasonably healthy, and not severely malnourished or on the edge of starvation (Hodges 1989). These findings point to the existence of exchange links that generally brought sufficient food supplies to the city."[704]"It was the only community in the region that could not have come close to producing its own food supply from its agricultural catchment. The city's founders likely did not expect a large proportion of the community's population to engage in farming, and they knew that they could depend on a supply of labor to carry water, food, and other materials to hilltop consumers."[705]

♠ provision of famine relief ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Skeletal analyses show that the city's Period I inhabitants were reasonably healthy, and not severely malnourished or on the edge of starvation (Hodges 1989). These findings point to the existence of exchange links that generally brought sufficient food supplies to the city."[706] "It was the only community in the region that could not have come close to producing its own food supply from its agricultural catchment. The city's founders likely did not expect a large proportion of the community's population to engage in farming, and they knew that they could depend on a supply of labor to carry water, food, and other materials to hilltop consumers."[707]

♠ Food storage ♣ ♥ "Monte Albán houses were somewhat more densely packed than those in other communities of the period. Judging from the smaller sizes of storage pits on average for Early I than for earlier phases (Winter 1974:982), perhaps Monte Albán's residents stored somewhat less food. In later periods, storage pits are not found (Winter 1974:983), probably indicating that households obtained their daily food supply from markets or through governmental institutions. [708] "No centralized storage facility associated with the central control of food or the caching of large amounts of portable wealth has been found, suggesting that the exchange system was not centered around or organized by a chief."[709] No evidence for centralised food storage has been found at Monte Alban.[710][711]

♠ provision of food storage ♣ ♥ "Monte Albán houses were somewhat more densely packed than those in other communities of the period. Judging from the smaller sizes of storage pits on average for Early I than for earlier phases (Winter 1974:982), perhaps Monte Albán's residents stored somewhat less food. In later periods, storage pits are not found (Winter 1974:983), probably indicating that households obtained their daily food supply from markets or through governmental institutions. [712] No evidence for centralised food storage has been found at Monte Alban.[713][714]

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ suspected unknown ♥ insufficient evidence

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Drinking water ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Evidence for Monte Alban architecture dating to this period is severely limited by later construction work from the MA II phase onwards.[715]

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Irrigation works ♣ inferred present ♥ "The series of civic water control features connecting the North Platform area with the North Barranca reservoir constitutes such an integrated system. The functions of drainage, collection, filtration, transport, and impoundment of water for domestic use are performed by specialized water control features. Independent construction of these specialized features is not likely since they are of little use when not operating as part of the complete system. This indicates a certain amount of central planning on the part of the Monte Alban elite. The planning of this system may have taken place as early as Late Monte Alban I (400-150 B.C.) since the North Platform was well underway by this time and a test excavation made by Kuttruff and Autry (1978) into the base of the diversionarydefensive wall which forms the reservoir in the North Barranca indicates a Late Monte Alban I construction date. Beginning of the system may be traced to the Early Monte Alban I phase (500-400 B.C.) since Caso, Bernal, and Acosta (1967: 96) report an Early Monte Alban I platform with a drain carved into bedrock in their North Platform excavations. While the degree of centralization needed to construct and maintain the system is unknown, the presence of a functionally complex water supply system, combined with the monumental architecture of the Main Plaza, indicate a centralized authority able to plan, mobilize, and coordinate labour on a large scale. Thus, the civic water supply system adds another dimension which must be considered in discussions about the classification of Monte Alban as urban or the centre of a military confederacy. Agricultural water control features are also present at Monte Alban. The most complex agricultural water control system is the dam and canal system which irrigated approximately 50 h. of the Xoxocotlan piedmont east of Monte Alban. This system was in use during Early and Late Monte Alban I (550-I50 B.C.) and was abandoned at the beginning of Monte Alban II. The agricultural system is smaller and less complex than the civic system and probably could have been managed and maintained by the population which lived along the canal. It has been estimated that the Xoxocotlan piedmont agricultural system could have fed, at most, 250 people (O'Brien, et al. i979; Mason, et al. I977). The civic water control system, however, probably supplied water to a substantial percentage of the population of Monte Alban, estimated by Blanton (1978: 30) to have been over 15,000 during the Classic period."[716] "As early as the San José phase, maize farmers in the Valley of Oaxaca supplemented water from rainfall with simple irrigation techniques (Flannery et al. 1967). Their repertoire included pot or well irrigation - a labor-intensive strategy that can be employed in many alluvial zones of the valley. One aspect of well irrigation is that it can be implemented by a single farmer or the members of one household. Early Oaxaca farmers also likely secured water by building modest check dams and small channels to divert water from seasonal runoffs and the temporary streams that run during the rainy season. These irrigation techniques also required no central management and little or no labor from outside the household. Neither large-scale irrigation systems nor massive networks of canals have been important during the long history of this region."[717] The use of small scale canal irrigation systems is inferred from the presence of settlements in the piedmont area (which would require some water control), but direct evidence for irrigation is found in the later periods. Any irrigation that was present would have been very small in extent and therefore not centrally controlled.[718][719][720]

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[733] It has been suggested that Monte Alban became the new centre of a confederation of people from different settlements of the valley, but the exact relationship between Monte Alban and the remaining settlements of the valley is not known.[734]

♠ Staple ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The establishment of a confederation (although not yet a state) at the beginning of this period is suggested by the simultaneous abandonment of settlements and founding of Monte Albán, which itself was internally divided into different groupings of people (as shown by distinct pottery assemblages).[735]

♠ Currency ♣ absent ♥ Monetary items have not been found dating to this period.[737]
